	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Methodist Church of Great Britain</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

18th&#160;century</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Methodist Church of Great Britain</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_denominations" title="List of Christian denominations">Christian denomination</a> in Britain and the <a href="/wiki/Mother_church" title="Mother church">mother church</a> to <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodists</a> worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It participates in the <a href="/wiki/World_Methodist_Council" title="World Methodist Council">World Methodist Council</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Council_of_Churches" title="World Council of Churches">World Council of Churches</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical">ecumenical</a> associations.
</p><p>Methodism began primarily through the work of <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a> (1703–1791), who led an <a href="/wiki/Evangelical" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical">evangelical</a> <a href="/wiki/Revivalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Revivalism">revival</a> in 18th-century Britain. An <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> priest, Wesley adopted unconventional and controversial practices, such as <a href="/wiki/Open-air_preaching" title="Open-air preaching">open-air preaching</a>, to reach factory labourers and newly urbanised masses uprooted from their traditional village culture at the start of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. His preaching centred upon the universality of God's <a href="/wiki/Prevenient_grace" title="Prevenient grace">grace</a> for all, the effect of faith on character and the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Christian_perfection" title="Christian perfection">perfection in love</a> during this life. He organised the new converts locally and in a "<a href="/wiki/Connexionalism" title="Connexionalism">Connexion</a>" across Britain.
</p><p>Following Wesley's death, the Methodist revival became a separate church and <a href="/wiki/Ordination" title="Ordination">ordained</a> its own <a href="/wiki/Minister_(Christianity)" title="Minister (Christianity)">ministers</a>; it is called a <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">nonconformist</a> church because it does not conform to the rules of the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">established</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. In the 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_Methodist_Church_(Great_Britain)" title="Wesleyan Methodist Church (Great Britain)">Wesleyan Methodist Church</a> experienced many secessions, with the largest of the offshoots being the <a href="/wiki/Primitive_Methodism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Primitive Methodism in the United Kingdom">Primitive Methodists</a>. The main streams of Methodism were <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Union" title="Methodist Union">reunited in 1932</a>, forming the Methodist Church as it is today.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Methodist_circuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist circuit">Methodist circuits</a>, containing several <a href="/wiki/Local_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Local church">local churches</a>, are gathered into thirty-one districts. The supreme governing body of the church is the annual Methodist Conference; it is headed by the <a href="/wiki/List_of_presidents_of_the_Methodist_Conference" title="List of presidents of the Methodist Conference">president of Conference</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Presbyter" title="Presbyter">presbyteral</a> minister, supported by a vice-president who can be a <a href="/wiki/Local_preacher" class="mw-redirect" title="Local preacher">local preacher</a> or <a href="/wiki/Deacon" title="Deacon">deacon</a>.
</p><p>The 2009 <a href="/wiki/British_Social_Attitudes_Survey" title="British Social Attitudes Survey">British Social Attitudes Survey</a> found that around 800,000 people, or 1.29 per cent of the British population, identified as Methodist.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In October 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, active membership stood at approximately 188,000,<sup id="cite_ref-Piggot_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piggot-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> representing a 7.45 per cent decline from the 2014 figure.<sup id="cite_ref-2014_statistics_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2014_statistics-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Methodism is the fourth-largest <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianity in the United Kingdom">Christian group in Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WCC-MCOGB_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCC-MCOGB-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Around 202,000 people attend a Methodist <a href="/wiki/Church_service" title="Church service">church service</a> each week, while 490,000 to 500,000 take part in some other form of Methodist activity, such as youth work and community events organised by local churches.<sup id="cite_ref-Piggot_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piggot-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NewInnHallSt_WesleyMemorialChurch_6058.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large, grey stone church with two entrances either side and a steeple on the right." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/NewInnHallSt_WesleyMemorialChurch_6058.jpg/220px-NewInnHallSt_WesleyMemorialChurch_6058.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/NewInnHallSt_WesleyMemorialChurch_6058.jpg/330px-NewInnHallSt_WesleyMemorialChurch_6058.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/NewInnHallSt_WesleyMemorialChurch_6058.jpg/440px-NewInnHallSt_WesleyMemorialChurch_6058.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="5184" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NewInnHallSt_WesleyMemorialChurch_6058.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wesley_Memorial_Church,_Oxford" title="Wesley Memorial Church, Oxford">Wesley Memorial Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>, the city where the Wesley brothers studied and formed the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Club" title="Holy Club">Holy Club</a>.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Methodism#Origins" title="Methodism">Methodism §&#160;Origins</a></div>
<p>The movement which would become the Methodist Church began in the mid-18th century within the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. A small group of students, including <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wesley" title="Charles Wesley">Charles Wesley</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Whitefield" title="George Whitefield">George Whitefield</a>, met at <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a>. They focused on methodical <a href="/wiki/Bible_study_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bible study (Christian)">study</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> and living a holy life. Other students mocked them, saying they were the "<a href="/wiki/Holy_Club" title="Holy Club">Holy Club</a>" and "the Methodists",<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> being methodical and exceptionally detailed in their Bible study, opinions and disciplined lifestyle.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica-Holy_Club_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-Holy_Club-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first Methodist movement outside the Church of England was associated with <a href="/wiki/Howell_Harris" title="Howell Harris">Howell Harris</a>, who led the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Methodist_revival" title="Welsh Methodist revival">Welsh Methodist revival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> This was to become the Calvinistic Methodist Church (today known as the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_of_Wales" title="Presbyterian Church of Wales">Presbyterian Church of Wales</a>). Another branch of the Methodist revival was under the ministry of George Whitefield, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Countess_of_Huntingdon%27s_Connexion" title="Countess of Huntingdon&#39;s Connexion">Countess of Huntingdon's Connexion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wesley%27s_Chapel_And_Statue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A bronze statue of John Wesley dressed in robes and preaching bands in the foreground, with a Georgian chapel in the background" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Wesley%27s_Chapel_And_Statue.jpg/220px-Wesley%27s_Chapel_And_Statue.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Wesley%27s_Chapel_And_Statue.jpg/330px-Wesley%27s_Chapel_And_Statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Wesley%27s_Chapel_And_Statue.jpg/440px-Wesley%27s_Chapel_And_Statue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="642" data-file-height="731" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wesley%27s_Chapel_And_Statue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wesley%27s_Chapel" title="Wesley&#39;s Chapel">Wesley's Chapel</a> was established by <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a> in 1778 to serve as his London base. Today it incorporates a museum of Methodism in its <a href="/wiki/Crypt" title="Crypt">crypt</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The largest branch of Methodism in England was organised by John Wesley; he took to <a href="/wiki/Open-air_preaching" title="Open-air preaching">open-air preaching</a> to recruit followers to his movement. He formed <a href="/wiki/Cell_group" title="Cell group">small classes</a> in which his followers would receive religious guidance and intensive accountability in their personal lives.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Wesley also appointed <a href="/wiki/Itinerant_preacher" title="Itinerant preacher">itinerant</a> evangelists to travel and preach as he did and to care for these groups of people. It is a tribute to Wesley's powers of oratory and organisational skills that the term <i>Methodism</i> is today assumed to mean <i>Wesleyan Methodism</i> unless otherwise specified.<sup id="cite_ref-welsh-chapels_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welsh-chapels-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Theologically, Wesley held to the "<a href="/wiki/Arminianism" title="Arminianism">Arminian</a>" view that <a href="/wiki/Salvation_in_Christianity" title="Salvation in Christianity">salvation</a> is available to all people,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> in contrast to the "<a href="/wiki/Calvinist" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a>" ideas of election and <a href="/wiki/Predestination_in_Calvinism" title="Predestination in Calvinism">predestination</a> that were accepted by the <a href="/wiki/Calvinistic_Methodists" title="Calvinistic Methodists">Calvinistic Methodists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-welsh-chapels_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welsh-chapels-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Methodist <a href="/wiki/Preacher" title="Preacher">preachers</a> were famous for their <a href="/wiki/Enthusiastic" class="mw-redirect" title="Enthusiastic">enthusiastic</a> sermons.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup> During Wesley's lifetime, many members of England's established church feared that new doctrines promulgated by the Methodists, such as the necessity of a <a href="/wiki/New_birth" class="mw-redirect" title="New birth">new birth</a> for salvation, of <a href="/wiki/Justification_by_faith" class="mw-redirect" title="Justification by faith">justification by faith</a> and of the constant and sustained action of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> upon the believer's soul, would produce ill effects upon weak minds. <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Evans" title="Theophilus Evans">Theophilus Evans</a>, an early critic of the movement, even wrote that it was "the natural Tendency of their Behaviour, in Voice and Gesture and horrid Expressions, to make People mad".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In one of his prints, <a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">William Hogarth</a> likewise attacked Methodists as "enthusiasts" full of "<a href="/wiki/Credulity,_Superstition,_and_Fanaticism" title="Credulity, Superstition, and Fanaticism">Credulity, Superstition, and Fanaticism</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Other attacks against the Methodists were physically violent—Wesley was nearly murdered by a mob at <a href="/wiki/Wednesbury" title="Wednesbury">Wednesbury</a> in 1743.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The Methodists responded vigorously to their critics and thrived despite the attacks against them.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Wesley_preaching_outside_a_church._Engraving._Wellcome_V0006868.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Engraving of Wesley standing on a plinth and preaching to a crowd." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/John_Wesley_preaching_outside_a_church._Engraving._Wellcome_V0006868.jpg/220px-John_Wesley_preaching_outside_a_church._Engraving._Wellcome_V0006868.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/John_Wesley_preaching_outside_a_church._Engraving._Wellcome_V0006868.jpg/330px-John_Wesley_preaching_outside_a_church._Engraving._Wellcome_V0006868.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/John_Wesley_preaching_outside_a_church._Engraving._Wellcome_V0006868.jpg/440px-John_Wesley_preaching_outside_a_church._Engraving._Wellcome_V0006868.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2784" data-file-height="2288" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Wesley_preaching_outside_a_church._Engraving._Wellcome_V0006868.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Wesley preaching outside a church. A 19th century engraving. Methodists were forbidden from preaching in <a href="/wiki/Parish_church" title="Parish church">parish churches</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>As Wesley and his colleagues preached around the country they formed local <a href="/wiki/Governance_of_the_Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain" title="Governance of the Methodist Church of Great Britain">societies</a>, authorised and organised through Wesley's leadership and conferences of preachers. Wesley insisted that Methodists regularly attend their local <a href="/wiki/Parish_church" title="Parish church">parish church</a> as well as Methodist meetings.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In 1784, Wesley made provision for the continuance as a corporate body after his death of the 'Yearly Conference of the People called Methodists'.<sup id="cite_ref-Birth-Conference_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birth-Conference-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> He nominated 100 people and declared them to be its members and laid down the method by which their successors were to be appointed. The Conference has remained the governing body of Methodism ever since.<sup id="cite_ref-Birth-Conference_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birth-Conference-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Separation_from_the_Church_of_England">Separation from the Church of England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Separation from the Church of England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_First_Methodist_chapel_called_%22The_Foundry%22_-_Capel_Cyntaf_y_Methodistiaid_Wesleyaidd_a_Adnabyddid_Wrth_%22Y_Foundry%22.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/The_First_Methodist_chapel_called_%22The_Foundry%22_-_Capel_Cyntaf_y_Methodistiaid_Wesleyaidd_a_Adnabyddid_Wrth_%22Y_Foundry%22.jpeg/220px-The_First_Methodist_chapel_called_%22The_Foundry%22_-_Capel_Cyntaf_y_Methodistiaid_Wesleyaidd_a_Adnabyddid_Wrth_%22Y_Foundry%22.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/The_First_Methodist_chapel_called_%22The_Foundry%22_-_Capel_Cyntaf_y_Methodistiaid_Wesleyaidd_a_Adnabyddid_Wrth_%22Y_Foundry%22.jpeg/330px-The_First_Methodist_chapel_called_%22The_Foundry%22_-_Capel_Cyntaf_y_Methodistiaid_Wesleyaidd_a_Adnabyddid_Wrth_%22Y_Foundry%22.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/The_First_Methodist_chapel_called_%22The_Foundry%22_-_Capel_Cyntaf_y_Methodistiaid_Wesleyaidd_a_Adnabyddid_Wrth_%22Y_Foundry%22.jpeg/440px-The_First_Methodist_chapel_called_%22The_Foundry%22_-_Capel_Cyntaf_y_Methodistiaid_Wesleyaidd_a_Adnabyddid_Wrth_%22Y_Foundry%22.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="733" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_First_Methodist_chapel_called_%22The_Foundry%22_-_Capel_Cyntaf_y_Methodistiaid_Wesleyaidd_a_Adnabyddid_Wrth_%22Y_Foundry%22.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The first Methodist chapel called "<a href="/wiki/The_Foundery" title="The Foundery">The Foundery</a>"</i>. Lithograph by  H. Humphreys, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1865</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>As his societies multiplied, and elements of an <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_polity" title="Ecclesiastical polity">ecclesiastical system</a> were successively adopted, the breach between Wesley and the Church of England (<a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>) gradually widened. In 1784, Wesley responded to the shortage of priests in the American colonies due to the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> by ordaining preachers for America with power to administer the <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacraments</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-split_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-split-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Wesley's actions precipitated the split between American Methodists and the Church of England (which held that only bishops could ordain persons to ministry).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With regard to the position of Methodism within <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>, "John Wesley once noted that what God had achieved in the development of Methodism was no mere human endeavor but the work of God. As such it would be preserved by God so long as history remained."<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Calling it "the grand depositum" of the Methodist faith, Wesley specifically taught that the propagation of the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Entire_sanctification" class="mw-redirect" title="Entire sanctification">entire sanctification</a> was the reason that God raised up the Methodists in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-DaviesGeorge2017_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DaviesGeorge2017-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>British Methodism separated from the Church of England soon after the death of Wesley. There were early contentions over the powers of preachers and the Conference, and the timing of chapel services.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker2008_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker2008-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> At this point in time a majority of Methodist members were not attending Anglican church services.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker2008_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker2008-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The 1795 Plan of Pacification permitted Methodist chapels to celebrate Holy Communion where both a majority of trustees and a majority of the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/steward" class="extiw" title="wikt:steward">stewards</a> and leaders allowed it.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner2014_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2014-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> (These services often used <a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Service_of_the_Methodists" title="The Sunday Service of the Methodists">Wesley's abridgement</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner2014_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2014-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>) This permission was later extended to the administration of baptism, burial and timing of services, bringing Methodist chapels into direct competition with the local parish church. Consequently, known Methodists were excluded from the Church of England.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker2008_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker2008-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Kilham" title="Alexander Kilham">Alexander Kilham</a> and his "radicals" denounced the Conference for giving too much power to the ministers of the church at the expense of the <a href="/wiki/Laity" title="Laity">laity</a>. In 1797, following the Plan of Pacification, Kilham was expelled from the church. The radicals formed the <a href="/wiki/Methodist_New_Connexion" title="Methodist New Connexion">Methodist New Connexion</a>, while the original body came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_Methodist_Church_(Great_Britain)" title="Wesleyan Methodist Church (Great Britain)">Wesleyan Methodist Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker2008_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker2008-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1790_to_1910">1790 to 1910</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1790 to 1910">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Methodist_Family_Tree_Diagram_Vertical.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Methodist_Family_Tree_Diagram_Vertical.svg/330px-Methodist_Family_Tree_Diagram_Vertical.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="544" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Methodist_Family_Tree_Diagram_Vertical.svg/495px-Methodist_Family_Tree_Diagram_Vertical.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Methodist_Family_Tree_Diagram_Vertical.svg/660px-Methodist_Family_Tree_Diagram_Vertical.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="510" data-file-height="840" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Methodist_Family_Tree_Diagram_Vertical.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Timeline of Methodist connexions in Britain</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_growth">Early growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Early Methodists were systematic in collecting statistics on membership.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Their growth was rapid, from 58,000 in 1790 to 302,000 in 1830 and 518,000 in 1850.<sup id="cite_ref-Cannon_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cannon-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Those were the official members, but the national census of 1851 counted people with an informal connection to Methodism, and the total was 1,463,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Cannon_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cannon-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Growth was steady in both rural and urban areas, despite disruption caused by numerous <a href="/wiki/Schism" title="Schism">schisms</a>; these resulted in separate denominations (or "<a href="/wiki/Connexionalism" title="Connexionalism">connexions</a>") such as the Wesleyan Methodist Church, the first and largest, followed by the New Connexion, the <a href="/wiki/Bible_Christian_Church" title="Bible Christian Church">Bible Christian Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Primitive_Methodist_Church" title="Primitive Methodist Church">Primitive Methodist Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cannon_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cannon-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> (They would be eventually reunited in the <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Union" title="Methodist Union">Methodist Union</a> of 1932.)  Some of the growth can be attributed to the failure of the established Church of England to provide church facilities. A series of reforms<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2019)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in the established church helped to correct that failure, so there were fewer opportunities for the Nonconformists in general and the Methodists in particular to keep growing. Membership reached 602,000 in 1870 and peaked at 841,000 in 1910.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-David_Hempton_2005_p_214_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_Hempton_2005_p_214-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Early Methodism was particularly prominent in <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, which were key centers of activity by the Bible Christian faction.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The Bible Christians produced many preachers, and sent many missionaries to Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Methodism as a whole grew rapidly in the old mill towns of <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>, where the preachers stressed that the working classes were equal to the upper classes in the eyes of God.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In Wales, three elements separately welcomed Methodism: Welsh-speaking, English-speaking, and <a href="/wiki/Calvinistic_Methodism" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinistic Methodism">Calvinistic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The independent Methodist movement did not appeal to England's <a href="/wiki/Landed_gentry" title="Landed gentry">landed gentry</a>; they favoured the developing <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical revival">evangelical movement</a> inside the Church of England.  However, Methodism became popular among ambitious <a href="/wiki/Social_structure_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Social structure of the United Kingdom">middle class</a> families.<sup id="cite_ref-Binfield_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Binfield-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> For example, the Osborn family of Sheffield, whose <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Osborn_%26_Company" title="Samuel Osborn &amp; Company">steel company</a> emerged in the mid-19th century in Sheffield's period of rapid industrialisation. Historian Clyde Binfield says their fervent Methodist faith strengthened their commitment to economic independence, spiritual certainty and civic responsibility.<sup id="cite_ref-Binfield_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Binfield-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Methodism was especially popular among skilled workers and much less prevalent among labourers. Historians such as <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lie_Hal%C3%A9vy" title="Élie Halévy">Élie Halévy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_J._Hobsbawm" class="mw-redirect" title="Eric J. Hobsbawm">Eric J. Hobsbawm</a> and <a href="/wiki/E._P._Thompson" title="E. P. Thompson">E. P. Thompson</a>, have explored the role of Methodism in the early decades of the making of the British <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> (1760–1820). On the one hand it provided a model of how to efficiently organise large numbers of people and sustain their connection over a long period of time, and on the other it diverted and discouraged <a href="/wiki/Political_radicalism" title="Political radicalism">political radicalism</a>. In explaining why Britain did not undergo a <a href="/wiki/Social_revolution" title="Social revolution">social revolution</a> in the period 1790-1832, a time that appeared ripe for violent social upheaval, Halévy argued that Methodism forestalled revolution among the working class by redirecting its energies toward spiritual affairs rather than workplace concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Thompson argues that overall it had a politically regressive effect.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership">Leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>John Wesley was the longtime president of the Methodist Conference, but after his death it was agreed that in future, so much authority would not be placed in the hands of one man. Instead, the president would be elected for one year, to sit in Wesley's chair.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Successive Methodist schisms resulted in multiple presidents, before a united conference assembled in 1932.
</p><p>Wesley wrote, edited or abridged some 400 publications. As well as theology he wrote about music, marriage, medicine, <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">abolitionism</a> and politics.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Wesley himself and the senior leadership were political conservatives. Although many <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade union</a> leaders were attracted to Methodism—the <a href="/wiki/Tolpuddle_Martyrs" title="Tolpuddle Martyrs">Tolpuddle Martyrs</a> being an early example—the church itself did not actively support the unions.  Historians Patrick O'Brien and Roland Quinault argue:
</p>
<dl><dd>John Wesley's own Tory sympathies and autocratic instincts had been strong and genuine, and as far as possible he had instilled into his followers deference toward established social and religious authorities. He emphasised political quietism. His mission he saw as strictly spiritual, and his own inherently conservative political instincts and social values reinforced a pragmatic concern to give as little offense as possible to a suspicious wider society. These same motives influenced the ministerial oligarchy...."Methodism" said Jabez Bunting...hates democracy as it hates sin."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jabez_Bunting" title="Jabez Bunting">Jabez Bunting</a> (1779–1858) was the most prominent leader of the Wesleyan Methodist movement after Wesley's death. He preached successful <a href="/wiki/Christian_revival" title="Christian revival">revivals</a> until 1802, when he saw revivals leading to dissension and division. He then became dedicated to church order and discipline, and vehemently opposed revivalism.<sup id="cite_ref-Ward_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> He was a popular preacher in numerous cities. He was four times chosen to be president of the Conference and held numerous senior positions as administrator and watched budgets very closely. Bunting and his allies centralised power by making the Conference the final arbiter of Methodism, and giving it the power to reassign preachers and select <a href="/wiki/Superintendent_(ecclesiastical)" title="Superintendent (ecclesiastical)">superintendents</a>. He was zealous in the cause of foreign missions.  In English politics he was conservative. He had little tolerance for liberal elements or for Sunday schools and temperance crusades, which led to expulsion of his opponents, whereupon a third of the members broke away in 1849. Numerous alliances with other groups failed and weakened his control.<sup id="cite_ref-Ward_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/w/index.php?title=William_Bramwell&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="William Bramwell (page does not exist)">William Bramwell</a> was a preacher who engendered controversy due to his intense revivalist preaching style, which spurred awakenings throughout the north of England—including the 1793-97 Yorkshire Revival—and his association with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Kilham" title="Alexander Kilham">Alexander Kilham</a> (1762-1798). Kilham was a revivalist who led the New Connexion secession from mainstream Wesleyan ministry.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugh_Price_Hughes3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Hugh_Price_Hughes3.jpg/180px-Hugh_Price_Hughes3.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Hugh_Price_Hughes3.jpg/270px-Hugh_Price_Hughes3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/Hugh_Price_Hughes3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="327" data-file-height="454" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugh_Price_Hughes3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hugh Price Hughes, editor and orator, encouraged Methodists to support the more moralistic <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hugh_Price_Hughes" title="Hugh Price Hughes">Hugh Price Hughes</a> (1847–1902) was the first superintendent of the <a href="/wiki/West_London_Methodist_Mission" title="West London Methodist Mission">West London Methodist Mission</a>, a key Methodist organisation. Recognised as one of the greatest orators of his era, he also founded and edited an influential newspaper, the <i>Methodist Times</i> in 1885.  Hughes played a key role in leading Methodists into the Liberal Party coalition, away from the Conservative leanings of previous Methodist leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Scott_Lidgett" title="John Scott Lidgett">John Scott Lidgett</a> (1854–1953) achieved prominence both as a theologian and reformer by stressing the importance of the church's engagement with the whole of society and human culture. He promoted the <a href="/wiki/Social_Gospel" title="Social Gospel">Social Gospel</a> and founded the <a href="/wiki/Bermondsey_Settlement" title="Bermondsey Settlement">Bermondsey Settlement</a> to reach the poor of London, as well as the "<a href="/wiki/Wesley_Guild" title="Wesley Guild">Wesley Guild</a>", a social organisation aimed at young people which reached 150,000 members by 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Early Methodism experienced a radical and spiritual phase that allowed women authority in church leadership. The role of the woman preacher emerged from the sense that the home should be a place of community care and should foster personal growth. Women gained self-esteem at this time when members were encouraged to testify about the nature of their faith. Methodist women formed a community that cared for the vulnerable, extending the role of mothering beyond physical care.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>  However the centrality of women's role sharply diminished after 1790 as the church became more structured and more male dominated.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 18th century <a href="/wiki/Selina_Hastings,_Countess_of_Huntingdon" title="Selina Hastings, Countess of Huntingdon">Selina Hastings, Countess of Huntingdon</a> (1707–91), played a major role in financing and guiding early Methodism. Selina was the first female principal of a men's college in Wales, <a href="/wiki/Trefeca" title="Trefeca">Trevecca College</a>, for the education of Methodist ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-Orchard_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orchard-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> She financed the building of 64 chapels in England and Wales, wrote often to George Whitefield and John Wesley and funded mission work in colonial America. She is best remembered for her adversarial relationships with other Methodists who objected to a woman having power.<sup id="cite_ref-Orchard_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orchard-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Youth_and_education">Youth and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Youth and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Methodists placed a high priority on close guidance of their youth, as seen in the activities of <a href="/wiki/Sunday_school" title="Sunday school">Sunday schools</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Band_of_Hope" class="mw-redirect" title="Band of Hope">Band of Hope</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Temperance_movement" title="Temperance movement">temperance</a> organisation in which children took a pledge of total abstinence from alcohol).<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingswood_School_Chapel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Kingswood_School_Chapel.jpg/220px-Kingswood_School_Chapel.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Kingswood_School_Chapel.jpg/330px-Kingswood_School_Chapel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Kingswood_School_Chapel.jpg/440px-Kingswood_School_Chapel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingswood_School_Chapel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chapel of <a href="/wiki/Kingswood_School" title="Kingswood School">Kingswood School</a>, the world's oldest Methodist educational institution<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Wesley himself opened schools at <a href="/wiki/The_Foundery" title="The Foundery">The Foundery</a> in London, and <a href="/wiki/Kingswood_School" title="Kingswood School">Kingswood School</a>. A Wesleyan report in 1832 said that for the church to prosper the system of Sunday schools should be augmented by day-schools with educated teachers. It was proposed in 1843 that 700 new day-schools be established within seven years. Though a steady increase was achieved, that ambitious target could not be reached, in part limited by the number of suitably qualified teachers. Most teachers came from one institution in Glasgow. The Wesleyan Education Report for 1844 called for a permanent Wesleyan teacher-training college. The result was the foundation of <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Training_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Training College">Westminster Training College</a> at Horseferry Road, <a href="/wiki/City_of_Westminster" title="City of Westminster">Westminster</a> in 1851.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>19th century England lacked a state school system; the major supplier was the Church of England. The Wesleyan Education Committee, which existed from 1838 to 1902, has documented Methodism's involvement in the education of children. At first most effort was placed in creating Sunday schools. In 1832 there were 3,339 Sunday schools with 59,277 teachers and 341,442 pupils.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In 1836 the Wesleyan Methodist Conference gave its blessing to the creation of "Weekday schools".<sup id="cite_ref-education_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-education-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> In 1902 the Methodists operated 738 schools, so their children would not have to learn from Anglican teachers. The Methodists, along with other Nonconformists, bitterly opposed the <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1902" title="Education Act 1902">Education Act of 1902</a>, which funded Church of England schools, and funded Methodists schools too but placed them under <a href="/wiki/Local_education_authorities" class="mw-redirect" title="Local education authorities">local education authorities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In the 20th century the number of Methodist Church-operated schools declined, as many became state-run schools, with only 28 still operating in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_missions">Colonial missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Colonial missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Through vigorous <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary</a> work, Methodism spread throughout the British Empire. It was especially successful in the new United States, thanks to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Great_Awakening" title="Second Great Awakening">Second Great Awakening</a> of the early 19th century. English emigrants brought Methodism to Canada and Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> British and American missionaries reached out to India and some other imperial colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In general the conversion efforts were only modestly successful, but reports back to Britain did have an influence in shaping how Methodists understood the wider world.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nonconformist_conscience">Nonconformist conscience</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nonconformist conscience">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist_conscience" title="Nonconformist conscience">Nonconformist conscience</a></div>
<p>Historians group Methodists together with other Protestant groups as "Nonconformists" or <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenter" class="mw-redirect" title="English Dissenter">Dissenters</a>, standing in opposition to the established Church of England. In the 19th century the Dissenters who went to chapel comprised half the people who actually attended services on Sunday. The "Nonconformist conscience" was their moral sensibility which they tried to implement in British politics.<sup id="cite_ref-Bebbington_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bebbington-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The two categories of Dissenters, or Nonconformists, were in addition to the evangelicals or "<a href="/wiki/Low_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Low Church">Low Church</a>" element in the Church of England. "Old Dissenters", dating from the 16th and 17th centuries, included <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Congregationalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Congregationalists">Congregationalists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_of_Unitarian_and_Free_Christian_Churches" title="General Assembly of Unitarian and Free Christian Churches">Unitarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Presbyterians" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterians">Presbyterians</a> outside Scotland. "New Dissenters" emerged in the 18th century and were mainly Methodists, especially the Wesleyan Methodists.<sup id="cite_ref-Bebbington_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bebbington-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The "Nonconformist conscience" of the "Old" group emphasised <a href="/wiki/Religious_freedom" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious freedom">religious freedom</a> and equality, pursuit of justice, and opposition to discrimination, compulsion and coercion. The "New Dissenters" (and also the Anglican evangelicals) stressed personal morality issues, including sexuality, temperance, family values and <a href="/wiki/Sabbath" title="Sabbath">Sabbath</a>-keeping. Both factions were politically active, but until the mid-19th century the Old group supported mostly <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whigs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberals</a> in politics, while the New generally supported <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a>. However the Methodists changed and in the 1880s moved into the Liberal Party, drawn in large part by Gladstone's intense moralism. The result was a merging of the Old and New, strengthening their great weight as a political pressure group.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> They joined together on new issues especially supporting temperance and opposing the <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1902" title="Education Act 1902">Education Act 1902</a>, with the former of special interest to Methodists.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>  By 1914 the conscience was weakening and by the 1920s it was virtually dead politically.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_(Taken_by_Flickr_user_30th_June_2012).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_%28Taken_by_Flickr_user_30th_June_2012%29.jpg/180px-Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_%28Taken_by_Flickr_user_30th_June_2012%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_%28Taken_by_Flickr_user_30th_June_2012%29.jpg/270px-Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_%28Taken_by_Flickr_user_30th_June_2012%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_%28Taken_by_Flickr_user_30th_June_2012%29.jpg/360px-Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_%28Taken_by_Flickr_user_30th_June_2012%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3061" data-file-height="4289" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_(Taken_by_Flickr_user_30th_June_2012).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The octagonal Methodist chapel in <a href="/wiki/Heptonstall" title="Heptonstall">Heptonstall</a> is one of the oldest in England.</div></div></div>
<p>In the early days of Methodism chapels were sometimes built octagonal, largely to avoid conflict with the established Church of England. The first was in <a href="/wiki/Norwich" title="Norwich">Norwich</a> (1757); it was followed by <a href="/wiki/Rotherham" title="Rotherham">Rotherham</a> (1761), <a href="/wiki/Whitby" title="Whitby">Whitby</a> (1762), <a href="/wiki/Yarm" title="Yarm">Yarm</a> (1763), <a href="/wiki/Heptonstall" title="Heptonstall">Heptonstall</a> (1764) and nine others. John Wesley personally approved the design of the octagonal chapels, stating, "It is better for the voice and on many accounts more commodious than any other." He is also said to have added—"there are no corners for the devil to hide in".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Methodist Heritage</i> records the Yarm chapel as the oldest in England in continual use.<sup id="cite_ref-MethHeritage_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MethHeritage-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Its design and construction were overseen by Wesley, who preached at the chapel frequently and declared it as his "favourite".<sup id="cite_ref-MethHeritage_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MethHeritage-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, the Heptonstall chapel has also contested for the title of oldest octagon chapel in continual use.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The building featured in the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> television series <i>Churches: How to Read Them</i>. Presenter <a href="/wiki/Richard_Taylor_(British_writer)" title="Richard Taylor (British writer)">Richard Taylor</a> named it as one of his ten favourite churches, saying: "If buildings have an aura, this one radiated friendship."<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primitive_Methodism">Primitive Methodism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Primitive Methodism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Primitive_Methodism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Primitive Methodism in the United Kingdom">Primitive Methodism in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<p>The Wesleyan Methodists' rejection of <a href="/wiki/Christian_revival" title="Christian revival">revivals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camp_meeting" title="Camp meeting">camp meetings</a> led to the founding in 1820 of the Primitive Methodist Connexion in England and Scotland, which emphasised those practices. It was a democratic, <a href="/wiki/Laity" title="Laity">lay</a>-oriented movement. Its social base was among the poorer members of society; they appreciated both its content (damnation, salvation, sinners and saints) and style (direct, spontaneous, and passionate). It offered an alternative to the more middle class Wesleyan Methodists and the upper class controlled Anglican established church, and in turn sometimes led adherents to <a href="/wiki/Pentecostalism" title="Pentecostalism">Pentecostalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The Primitive Methodists were poorly funded and had trouble building chapels or schools, and supporting ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Growth was strong in the middle 19th century. Membership declined after 1900 because of growing secularism in society, a resurgence of Anglicanism among the working classes, competition from other Nonconformist denominations (including William Booth's <a href="/wiki/Salvation_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Salvation Army">Salvation Army</a>), and competition among different Methodist branches.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The leading theologian of the Primitive Methodists was <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Peake" title="Arthur Peake">Arthur Peake</a> (1865–1929), professor of biblical criticism at the University of Manchester, 1904-29. He was active in numerous leadership roles and promoted <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Union" title="Methodist Union">Methodist Union</a> that came about in 1932 after his death. He popularised modern biblical scholarship, including the new <a href="/wiki/Higher_criticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Higher criticism">higher criticism</a>. He approached the Bible not as the <a href="/wiki/Infallible" class="mw-redirect" title="Infallible">infallible</a> word of God, but as the record of revelation written by fallible humans.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1910_to_present">1910 to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 1910 to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reunification">Reunification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Reunification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Membership of the various Methodist branches peaked at 841,000 in 1910, then fell steadily to 425,000 in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-David_Hempton_2005_p_214_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_Hempton_2005_p_214-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The second half of the 19th century saw many of the small schisms reunited to become the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Free_Churches" title="United Methodist Free Churches">United Methodist Free Churches</a> and a further union in 1907 with the Methodist New Connexion and Bible Christian Church brought the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church_(Great_Britain)" title="United Methodist Church (Great Britain)">United Methodist Church</a> into being. In 1908 the major three branches were the <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_Methodist_Church_(Great_Britain)" title="Wesleyan Methodist Church (Great Britain)">Wesleyan Methodists</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Primitive_Methodism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Primitive Methodism in the United Kingdom">Primitive Methodists</a> and the United Methodists. After the late 19th century evangelical approaches to the unchurched were less effective and less used. Methodists paid more attention to their current membership, and less to outreach, while middle-class family size shrank steadily.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> There were fewer famous preachers or outstanding leaders. The theological change that emphasised the conversion experience as being a one-time lifetime event rather than as a step on the road to perfection lessened the importance of the class meeting and made revivals less meaningful.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The growth mechanisms that had worked so well in the expansion phase in the early 19th century were largely discarded, including revivals and the personal appeal to decide for Christ class meetings, as well as the love feast, the Sunday night prayer-meeting, and the open-air meeting. The failure to grow was signaled by the flagging experience of the Sunday schools, whose enrollments fell steadily.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HymnBook-wyrdlight.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/HymnBook-wyrdlight.jpg/220px-HymnBook-wyrdlight.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/HymnBook-wyrdlight.jpg/330px-HymnBook-wyrdlight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/HymnBook-wyrdlight.jpg/440px-HymnBook-wyrdlight.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HymnBook-wyrdlight.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>Methodist Hymn-Book</i> (a 1st edition, pictured) was printed in 1933 to commemorate the union of the three major Methodist branches.</div></div></div>
<p>With the <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Union" title="Methodist Union">Methodist Union</a> of 1932 the three main Methodist connexions in Britain—the Wesleyans, Primitive Methodists and United Methodist Church—came together to form the present Methodist Church.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Methodist_Church_Act_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Methodist_Church_Act-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Some offshoots of Methodism, such as the <a href="/wiki/Salvation_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Salvation Army">Salvation Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Nazarene" title="Church of the Nazarene">Church of the Nazarene</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Methodist_Connexion" title="Independent Methodist Connexion">Independent Methodist Connexion</a>, remain totally separate organisations.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attempts_to_reverse_the_decline">Attempts to reverse the decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Attempts to reverse the decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Reginald Ward states that because the union of 1932 was unevenly implemented until the 1950s, it distracted attention away from the urgent need to revive the fast-shrinking movement. The hoped-for financial gains proved to be illusory, and Methodist leaders spent the early <a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Social history of the United Kingdom (1945–present)">post-war era</a> vainly trying to achieve union with the Church of England.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Multiple approaches were used to turn around the membership decline and flagging zeal in the post-war era, but none worked well. For example, Methodist <a href="/wiki/Group_tour" class="mw-redirect" title="Group tour">group tours</a> were organised, but they ended when it was clear they made little impact.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 20th century Methodists increasingly embraced <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">socialist</a> ideas. <a href="/wiki/Donald_Soper,_Baron_Soper" title="Donald Soper, Baron Soper">Donald Soper</a> (1903–1998) was perhaps the most widely recognised Methodist leader. An activist, he promoted pacifism and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_disarmament" title="Nuclear disarmament">nuclear disarmament</a> in cooperation with the Labour Party. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> was a moralistic Methodist; Soper denounced her policies as unchristian. However, in "the battle for Britain's soul" she was reelected over and over.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>  Historian Martin Wellings says of Soper:
</p>
<dl><dd>His combination of modernist theology, high sacramentalism, and Socialist politics, expressed with insouciant wit and unapologetic élan, thrilled audiences, delighted admirers, and reduced opponents to apoplectic fury.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>In 1967, Soper, then the only Methodist minister in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, lamented that:
</p>
<dl><dd>To-day we are living in what is the first genuinely pagan age—that is to say, there are so many people, particularly children, who never remember having heard hymns at their mother's knee, as I have, whose first tunes are from <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_1" title="BBC Radio 1">Radio One</a>, and not from any hymn book; whose first acquaintance with their friends and relations and other people is not in the Sunday School or in the Church at all, as mine was.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Scholars have suggested multiple possible reasons for the decline, but have not agreed on their relative importance. Wellings lays out the "classical model" of <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularization</a>, while noting that it has been challenged by some scholars.
</p>
<dl><dd>The familiar starting-point, a classical model of secularization, argues that religious faith becomes less plausible and religious practice more difficult in advanced industrial and urbanized societies. The breakdown or disruption of traditional communities and norms of behavior; the spread of a scientific world-view diminishing the scope of the supernatural and the role of God; increasing material affluence promoting self-reliance and this-worldly optimism; and greater awareness and toleration of different creeds and ideas, encouraging religious pluralism and eviscerating commitment to a particular faith, all form components of the case for secularization. Applied to the British churches in general by Steve Bruce and to Methodism in particular by Robert Currie, this model traces decline back to the Victorian era and charts in the twentieth century a steady ebbing of the sea of faith.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Over the ten-year period from 2006 to 2016 membership decreased from 262,972 to 188,398. This represents a decline at a rate of 3.5 per cent year-on-year.<sup id="cite_ref-Piggot_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piggot-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship_and_liturgy">Worship and liturgy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Worship and liturgy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Methodist_Communion.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Methodist_Communion.jpg/220px-Methodist_Communion.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Methodist_Communion.jpg/330px-Methodist_Communion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/80/Methodist_Communion.jpg 2x" data-file-width="408" data-file-height="461" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Methodist_Communion.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A minister, dressed in a <a href="/wiki/Cassock" title="Cassock">cassock</a> with <a href="/wiki/Bands_(neckwear)" title="Bands (neckwear)">bands</a>, presides over a service of Holy Communion</div></div></div>
<p>Methodism was endowed by the Wesley brothers with <a href="/wiki/Christian_worship" title="Christian worship">worship</a> characterised by a twofold practice: the <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">ritual</a> <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a> of the Anglican <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a></i> on the one hand and the simple <a href="/wiki/Preaching" class="mw-redirect" title="Preaching">preaching</a> service on the other.<sup id="cite_ref-Firth_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firth-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Listening to the reading of Scripture and a sermon based upon the biblical text is virtually always included in Methodist worship. The Methodist Church follows the <a href="/wiki/Revised_Common_Lectionary" title="Revised Common Lectionary">Revised Common Lectionary</a>, in common with other major denominations in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Similar to most historic Christian churches, the Methodist Church has official liturgies for services such as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Communion" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Communion">Holy Communion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ordination" title="Ordination">Ordination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">Marriage</a>. These and other patterns of worship are contained in the <i>Methodist Worship Book</i>, the most recent Methodist <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_book" title="Liturgical book">service book</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bradshaw2013_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradshaw2013-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> It states in its preface that worship is "a gracious encounter between God and the Church. God speaks to us, especially through scripture read and proclaimed and through symbols and sacraments. We respond chiefly through hymns and prayers and acts of dedication."<sup id="cite_ref-Worship_Book_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worship_Book-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Methodism has generally allowed for freedom in how the liturgy is celebrated—the <i>Worship Book</i> serves as a guideline, but ministers, preachers and other worship leaders are not bound to use it.<sup id="cite_ref-Worship_Book_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worship_Book-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_interior_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1241460.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_interior_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1241460.jpg/220px-Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_interior_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1241460.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_interior_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1241460.jpg/330px-Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_interior_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1241460.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_interior_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1241460.jpg/440px-Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_interior_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1241460.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="505" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heptonstall_Methodist_Church_interior_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1241460.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sanctuary of Heptonstall Methodist chapel. The prominent positioning of the pulpit reflects the emphasis on preaching as the central focus of most services.</div></div></div>
<p>The Methodist Church has used a succession of <a href="/wiki/Hymnal" title="Hymnal">hymnals</a> and service books. The <i>Methodist Hymn-Book</i> (1933) was the first hymnal published after the 1932 union.<sup id="cite_ref-Bradshaw2013_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradshaw2013-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> In 1936 the church authorised the <i>Book of Offices</i>, including an <i>Order for Morning Prayer</i>,  which closely replicated the <i>Book of Common Prayer</i> (1662).<sup id="cite_ref-Firth_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firth-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Later, the <i>Methodist Service Book</i> (1975) modernised the language used in the Communion prayers; its widespread usage has been cited as a cause for increased frequency of Communion celebration in the Methodist Church.<sup id="cite_ref-Dixon_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dixon-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The publication of a new hymnal, <i><a href="/wiki/Hymns_and_Psalms" title="Hymns and Psalms">Hymns and Psalms</a></i> (1983), expanded the repertoire of 20th century compositions.<sup id="cite_ref-Bradshaw2013_97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradshaw2013-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Methodist Worship Book</i> (1999) includes a wider range of services for every <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_year" title="Liturgical year">season</a>; it continues the 1975 service book's intention of preserving Methodist traditions while taking into account the insights of the <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_Movement" title="Liturgical Movement">liturgical renewal movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dixon_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dixon-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Its publication prompted minor controversy due to the utilisation of <a href="/wiki/Gender-neutral_language" title="Gender-neutral language">gender-neutral language</a> and the inclusion of a prayer addressed to "God our Father and our <i>Mother</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-Dixon_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dixon-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> This was seen by some as a "challenging" departure from the <a href="/wiki/Gender_of_God_in_Christianity" title="Gender of God in Christianity">masculine language</a> which is traditionally used when referring to God.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Methodism is known for its rich musical tradition, and Charles Wesley was instrumental in writing much of the <a href="/wiki/Hymnody" class="mw-redirect" title="Hymnody">hymnody</a> of the Methodist Church.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Singing_the_Faith" title="Singing the Faith">Singing the Faith</a></i> is the current hymnal, published by the church in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-StF_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StF-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> It contains 748 hymns and songs and 42 liturgical settings (such as the <a href="/wiki/Kyrie" title="Kyrie">Kyrie</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sanctus" title="Sanctus">Sanctus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord&#39;s Prayer">Lord's Prayer</a>, as well as material from the <a href="/wiki/Taiz%C3%A9_Community" title="Taizé Community">Taizé</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iona_Community" title="Iona Community">Iona</a> traditions).<sup id="cite_ref-StF_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StF-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> There are also 50 <a href="/wiki/Canticles" class="mw-redirect" title="Canticles">canticles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">psalms</a>, selected on the basis of their use within liturgy.<sup id="cite_ref-StF_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StF-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The collection of 89 hymns by Charles Wesley<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> is a reduction from over 200 in the 1933 <i>Hymn-Book</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bradshaw2013_97-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradshaw2013-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holy_Communion">Holy Communion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Holy Communion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Methodist congregations celebrate Holy Communion within a Sunday service generally at least once a month.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The practice of an '<a href="/wiki/Open_table" class="mw-redirect" title="Open table">open table</a>' is widespread in the Methodist Church. Although the phrasing and exact requirements in a particular local church may vary, generally all "who love the Lord Jesus Christ" are invited to receive <a href="/wiki/Sacramental_bread" title="Sacramental bread">bread</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sacramental_wine" title="Sacramental wine">wine</a>, irrespective of age or denominational identity. <i>Guidelines about Children and Holy Communion</i>, issued in 1987, affirmed that those receiving communion should, if not already baptised, be encouraged to be baptised—though acknowledging that this "theological principle" was not widely adhered to.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Covenant_Service">Covenant Service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Covenant Service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wesley_Covenant_Service" title="Wesley Covenant Service">Wesley Covenant Service</a></div>
<p>A distinctive liturgical feature of British Methodism is the Covenant Service. Methodists annually follow the call of John Wesley for a renewal of their <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(religion)" title="Covenant (religion)">covenant</a> with God.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In 1755, Wesley crafted the original Covenant Service using
material from the writings of eminent clerics <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Alleine" title="Joseph Alleine">Joseph</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Alleine" title="Richard Alleine">Richard Alleine</a>. In 1780, Wesley printed an excerpt from Richard Alleine's <i>Vindiciae Pietatis</i>, which is prayer for renewal of a believer's covenant with God.<sup id="cite_ref-Covenant_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Covenant-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> This excerpt, known in modified form as the Wesley Covenant Prayer, remained in use—linked with Holy Communion and observed on the first Sunday of the New Year—among Wesleyan Methodists until 1936.<sup id="cite_ref-Covenant_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Covenant-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In the 1920s, Wesleyan minister George B. Robson expanded the form of the Covenant Service by replacing most of the exhortation with prayers of adoration, thanksgiving and confession. Robson's Covenant Service was revised and officially authorised for use in the <i>Book of Offices</i> (1936). Further revisions, strengthening the link with Communion and <a href="/wiki/Intercession" title="Intercession">intercession</a> for the wider church and the world, appeared in the <i>Service Book</i> (1975) and <i>Worship Book</i> (1999).<sup id="cite_ref-Covenant_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Covenant-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> This Covenant Prayer, which has been adopted by other Christian traditions, has been described as "a celebration of all that God has done and an affirmation that we give our lives and choices to God".<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
