<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrine">Doctrine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Doctrine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_beliefs">Core beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Core beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A summary of Methodist <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">doctrine</a> is contained in the <i>Catechism for the Use of the People Called Methodists</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Catechism_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catechism-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Some core beliefs that are affirmed by most Methodists include:
</p>
<ul><li>The belief that <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God</a> is <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">all-knowing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnibenevolence" title="Omnibenevolence">possesses infinite love</a>, is <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">all-powerful</a>, and the creator of all things.</li>
<li>God has <a href="/wiki/God_and_eternity" title="God and eternity">always existed</a> and will always continue to exist.</li>
<li>God is <a href="/wiki/Trinitarianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinitarianism">three persons in one</a>: <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">Father</a>, the <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">Son</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus Christ</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_in_Christianity" title="Holy Spirit in Christianity">Holy Spirit</a>.</li>
<li>God is the master of all creation and humans are meant to live in a holy <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_covenant_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Wesleyan covenant theology">covenant</a> with him. Humans have broken this covenant by their <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_sin" title="Christian views on sin">sins</a> but can be forgiven through the <a href="/wiki/Salvation_in_Christianity" title="Salvation in Christianity">saving</a> <a href="/wiki/Grace_in_Christianity" title="Grace in Christianity">grace</a> of Jesus Christ.</li>
<li>Jesus was <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">God on earth</a>, in the form of a man who was <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">crucified</a> for the sins of all people, and who was <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrected</a> to bring them the hope of eternal life.</li>
<li>God's pardon for guilty sinners is granted to and received through <a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">faith alone</a>, not by any acts such as good deeds.</li>
<li>The grace of God is seen by people through the work of the Holy Spirit in their lives and in their world. (Personal and social holiness.)</li>
<li>Close adherence to the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Scripture</a> is essential to the faith because Scripture is the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">word of God</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Supper" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord&#39;s Supper">Lord's Supper</a> (more commonly called Holy Communion) are the two <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacraments</a> instituted by Jesus:
<ul><li>Baptism is a sacrament involving submersion or sprinkling of water on a participant. It symbolises being brought into the community of faith; the sacrament requires a response of <a href="/wiki/Repentance" title="Repentance">repentance</a> and faith in Jesus Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-Catechism_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catechism-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The church practices <a href="/wiki/Infant_baptism" title="Infant baptism">infant baptism</a> in anticipation of a response to be made later in <a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">confirmation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The Lord's Supper is a sacrament in which participants eat bread and drink wine in memory of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Last Supper</a>. The <i>Catechism</i> states, "Jesus Christ is present with his worshipping people ... As they eat the bread and drink the wine, through the power of the Holy Spirit they receive him by faith and with thanksgiving."<sup id="cite_ref-Catechism_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catechism-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wesleyan_theology">Wesleyan theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Wesleyan theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_theology" title="Wesleyan theology">Wesleyan</a> tradition stands at a unique cross-roads between <a href="/wiki/Evangelical" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical">evangelical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacramental</a>, between liturgical and <a href="/wiki/Charismatic_movement" title="Charismatic movement">charismatic</a>, and between <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Catholic">Anglo-Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed">Reformed</a> theology and practice.<sup id="cite_ref-Marsh2006_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marsh2006-112">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> It has been characterised as <a href="/wiki/Arminianism" title="Arminianism">Arminian theology</a> with an emphasis on the work of the Holy Spirit to bring <a href="/wiki/Sanctification" title="Sanctification">holiness</a> into the life of the participating believer. The Methodist Church teaches the Arminian concepts of <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conditional_election" title="Conditional election">conditional election</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanctifying_grace" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanctifying grace">sanctifying grace</a>. John Wesley was perhaps the clearest English proponent of Arminianism.<sup id="cite_ref-StanglinMcCall2012_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanglinMcCall2012-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tillett1907_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tillett1907-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The church teaches that <a href="/wiki/Salvation_in_Christianity" title="Salvation in Christianity">salvation</a> is entirely a work of God alone with no work by which it can be earned (<a href="/wiki/Monergism" title="Monergism">monergism</a>), and that one cannot either turn to God nor believe unless God has first drawn a person and implanted the desire in their heart (the Wesleyan doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Prevenient_grace" title="Prevenient grace">prevenient grace</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>John Wesley believed that certain aspects of the Christian faith required special emphasis.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Wesleyan Methodist minister William Fitzgerald (1856–1931) summarised the core emphases of Wesleyan doctrine by using four statements that collectively are called the ‘Four Alls’.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> These are expressed:
</p>
<ul><li>All people need to be saved (<a href="/wiki/Total_depravity" title="Total depravity">total depravity</a>)</li>
<li>All people can be saved (<a href="/wiki/Unlimited_atonement" title="Unlimited atonement">unlimited atonement</a>)</li>
<li>All people can know they are saved (<a href="/wiki/Assurance_(theology)#John_Wesley_and_Methodism" title="Assurance (theology)">assurance of faith</a>)</li>
<li>All people can be saved to the uttermost (<a href="/wiki/Christian_perfection" title="Christian perfection">Christian perfection</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Methodists have emphasised the importance of scriptural holiness, which entails more than personal piety; love of God is always linked with love of neighbour, a passion for <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">justice</a> and renewal in the life of the world. John Wesley made much of the process of <a href="/wiki/Sanctification" title="Sanctification">sanctification</a>, occasionally even seeming to claim that individuals could achieve ‘Christian perfection’ in this life.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is a traditional position of the Methodist Church that any disciplined theological work calls for the careful use of <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> by which to understand God's action and will.<sup id="cite_ref-Marsh2006_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marsh2006-112">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> However, Methodists also look to <a href="/wiki/Christian_tradition" title="Christian tradition">Christian tradition</a> as a source of doctrine. Wesley himself believed that the living core of the Christian faith was revealed in the Bible as the sole foundational source. The centrality of <a href="/wiki/Scripture" class="mw-redirect" title="Scripture">Scripture</a> was so important for Wesley that he called himself "<a href="/wiki/Homo_unius_libri" title="Homo unius libri">a man of one book</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Methodism has also emphasised a personal experience of faith; this is linked to the Methodist doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Assurance_(theology)" title="Assurance (theology)">assurance</a>. These four elements taken together form the <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_Quadrilateral" title="Wesleyan Quadrilateral">Wesleyan Quadrilateral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scripture">Scripture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Scripture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to a conference report, <i>A Lamp for our Feet and A Light to our Path</i>, issued in 2001 British Methodists hold a range of views about the Bible:<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The Bible is the Word of God and is therefore <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">inerrant</a> (free of all error and entirely trustworthy in everything which it records) and has complete authority in all matters of theology and behavior….</li>
<li>The Bible's teaching about God, salvation and Christian living is entirely trustworthy. It cannot be expected, however, to provide entirely accurate, scientific or historical information….</li>
<li>The Bible is the essential foundation on which Christian faith and life are built. However, its teachings were formed in particular historical and cultural contexts, and must therefore be read in that light….</li>
<li>The Bible's teaching, while foundational and authoritative for Christians, needs to be interpreted by the church…. Church tradition is therefore high importance as a practical source of authority.</li>
<li>The Bible is one of the main ways in which God speaks to the believer… Much stress is placed on spiritual experience itself, which conveys its own compelling authority.</li>
<li>The Bible witnesses to God's revelation of himself through history and supremely through Jesus Christ. However, the Bible is not itself that revelation, but only the witness to it…. Reason, tradition and experience are as important as the biblical witnesses.</li>
<li>The Bible comprises a diverse and often contradictory collection of documents which represent the experiences of various people in various times and places. The Christian's task is to follow, in some way, the example of Christ. And to the extent that the Bible records evidence of his character and teaching it offers a useful resource.</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrinal_standards">Doctrinal standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Doctrinal standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Methodist Church understands itself to be part of the <a href="/wiki/One,_holy,_catholic,_and_apostolic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="One, holy, catholic, and apostolic Church">one, holy, catholic, and apostolic Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vision_Statement_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vision_Statement-123">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> It recognises the historic <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_creeds" title="Ecumenical creeds">ecumenical creeds</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Apostle%27s_Creed" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle&#39;s Creed">Apostle's Creed</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> which may be used in <a href="/wiki/Church_service" title="Church service">church services</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Methodist practices and interpretation of beliefs have evolved over time, these practices and beliefs can be traced to the writings, hymns and sermons of the church's founders,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> especially John Wesley and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wesley" title="Charles Wesley">Charles Wesley</a>. The Methodist Church does not possess a strict set of doctrines comparable to that of the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Confession">Westminster Confession</a>, but it does specify general doctrinal standards, as follows:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Methodist Church claims and cherishes its place in the Holy Catholic Church which is the Body of Christ. It rejoices in the inheritance of the apostolic faith and loyally accepts the fundamental principles of the historic creeds and of the Protestant Reformation. It ever remembers that in the providence of God Methodism was raised up to spread scriptural holiness through the land by the proclamation of the evangelical faith and declares its unfaltering resolve to be true to its divinely appointed mission.
</p><p>The doctrines of the evangelical faith which Methodism has held from the beginning and still holds are based upon the divine revelation recorded in the Holy Scriptures. The Methodist Church acknowledges this revelation as the supreme rule of faith and practice. These evangelical doctrines to which the preachers of the Methodist Church are pledged are contained in <i>Wesley's Notes on the New Testament</i> and the first four volumes of his sermons.
</p><p>
The Notes on the New Testament and the 44 Sermons are not intended to impose a system of formal or speculative theology on Methodist preachers, but to set up standards of preaching and belief which should secure loyalty to the fundamental truths of the gospel of redemption and ensure the continued witness of the Church to the realities of the Christian experience of salvation.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Deed of Union (1932) <sup id="cite_ref-deed_of_union_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deed_of_union-127">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Positions_on_social_and_moral_issues">Positions on social and moral issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Positions on social and moral issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_issues">Life issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Life issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Abortion_and_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Abortion and Christianity">Abortion and Christianity</a></div>
<p>The <a href="#Annual_Conference">Methodist Conference</a> statement of 1976 says that the termination of any form of human life cannot be regarded superficially.<sup id="cite_ref-1976_statement_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1976_statement-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The church has also stated that the "unborn human" should be accorded rights progressively as it develops through the stages of gestation, from embryo to fetus, culminating with full respect as an individual at birth.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The 1976 statement gives examples of circumstances in which <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> may be permissible; these include situations where the life or health of the mother is at risk, in cases of serious abnormality where the child is incapable of survival, and in cases where the right of the unborn child to be healthy and wanted may not be met.<sup id="cite_ref-1976_statement_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1976_statement-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The Methodist Church believes that its members should work toward the elimination of the need for abortion by advocating for social support for mothers. The conference statement argues that "abortion must not be regarded as an alternative to contraception", and disagrees with <a href="/wiki/Abortion_law" title="Abortion law">complete legalisation</a>, recommending that abortion "should remain subject to a legal framework and to responsible counselling and to medical judgement."<sup id="cite_ref-1976_statement_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1976_statement-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Within this legal framework, it advocates limiting elective abortions to 20 weeks of pregnancy.<sup id="cite_ref-Butler_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-130">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The church generally supported the <a href="/wiki/Abortion_Act_1967" title="Abortion Act 1967">Abortion Act 1967</a> that made abortion legal only under certain circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-Butler_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-130">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-1976_statement_128-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1976_statement-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> It also supports the use of "responsible contraception" and <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a> as ways to prevent unwanted pregnancies.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Methodist Church strongly opposes <a href="/wiki/Assisted_suicide" title="Assisted suicide">assisted suicide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a>: "The final stage of an illness is not one which need represent the ultimate defeat for the doctor or nurse, but a supreme opportunity to help the patient at many levels, including those relating to emotional and spiritual well-being ... Dedicated workers in this field of care, including specialised <a href="/wiki/Hospice" title="Hospice">hospices</a>, demonstrate that it is possible to deal with all the symptoms which cause problems to the patient ... Euthanasia, assisted dying – both are artificial precipitation of death. Many Christians believe this idea is wrong. An approach to death as outlined above makes euthanasia inappropriate and irrelevant."<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Methodist Church supported the campaign to abolish <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Capital punishment in the United Kingdom">capital punishment in the United Kingdom</a>, and since then has totally opposed its reintroduction.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexuality_and_marriage">Sexuality and marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Sexuality and marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality_and_Methodism" title="Homosexuality and Methodism">Homosexuality and Methodism</a></div>
<p>Within the Methodist Church members have a broad range of views about <a href="/wiki/Human_sexuality" title="Human sexuality">human sexuality</a>, relationships, and the <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_marriage" title="Christian views on marriage">purpose of marriage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sexuality_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sexuality-134">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The church condemns all practices of sexuality "which are promiscuous, exploitative or demeaning in any way".<sup id="cite_ref-derbyresolutions_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derbyresolutions-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> In his 1743 tract, <i>Thoughts on marriage and a single life</i>, John Wesley taught that the ability to live a <a href="/wiki/Single_person" title="Single person">single life</a> is given by God to all believers, although few people are able to accept this gift.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1993 the Methodist Conference met in Derby and passed six resolutions covering issues related with human sexuality (known as the "Derby Resolutions" or "1993 Resolutions"). Among these, the conference reaffirmed the traditional Christian teaching of "chastity for all outside marriage and fidelity within it".<sup id="cite_ref-derbyresolutions_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derbyresolutions-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> (The church defines marriage as a union between a man and a woman.<sup id="cite_ref-family92_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-family92-137">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>) The Derby Resolutions also agreed that the church "recognises, affirms and celebrates the participation and ministry of lesbians and gay men" and allows the <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_LGBT_Christian_clergy" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordination of LGBT Christian clergy">ordination of openly gay ministers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-derbyresolutions_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derbyresolutions-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Methodist Church has a mixed position on the <a href="/wiki/Blessing_of_same-sex_unions_in_Christian_churches" title="Blessing of same-sex unions in Christian churches">blessing of same-sex couples</a>. In 2005 the Methodist Conference meeting in Torquay decided to allow its ministers to bless same-sex relationships, subject to local approval.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-religioustolerance_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-religioustolerance-139">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> It affirmed that the church should be "welcoming and inclusive" and not turn people away because of their sexual orientation.<sup id="cite_ref-religioustolerance_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-religioustolerance-139">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> However, in 2006 the Methodist Conference decided not to allow formal blessings, although ministers were allowed to offer informal private prayers.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> The 2013 conference set up a working party to oversee a process of "deep reflection and discernment" before reporting back to the conference in 2016 with recommendations about whether the definition of marriage should be revised.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Subsequently, in 2016 the conference voted to "revisit" the church's position on <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>, with a mandate from members "expressing a desire to endorse same-sex relationships".<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> At present, although there are no formal services, "prayers of thanksgiving or celebration may be said, and there may be informal services of thanksgiving or celebration...'Blessings', would be a formal liturgy, and are not permitted."<sup id="cite_ref-sexuality_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sexuality-134">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The church has no rules prohibiting its members (including ministers) from entering into or remaining within a <a href="/wiki/Civil_partnership" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil partnership">civil partnership</a> or <a href="/wiki/Civil_marriage" title="Civil marriage">civil</a> same-sex marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> 
On 3 July 2019 the Methodist Conference voted by 247 votes to 48 in principle to permit same-sex marriages in Methodist premises by Methodist ministers—a final decision is due to be made at the July 2020 conference.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Regarding <a href="/wiki/Transgender" title="Transgender">transgender</a> people, the church has stated that, after <a href="/wiki/Transitioning_(transgender)" title="Transitioning (transgender)">transitioning</a>, transgender people "are permitted to marry [in a church ceremony] a person of the opposite gender under current marriage law".<sup id="cite_ref-sexuality_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sexuality-134">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Outcome</i> is a representative group of <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">lesbian, gay, bisexual and transgender</a> (LGBT) Methodists. It aims to strengthen the Methodist Church's position as an <a href="/wiki/LGBT-affirming_Christian_denominations" class="mw-redirect" title="LGBT-affirming Christian denominations">LGBT-affirming denomination</a>. The group wants the church to allow same-sex marriage ceremonies.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcohol">Alcohol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Alcohol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_alcohol#Methodism" title="Christian views on alcohol">Christian views on alcohol §&#160;Methodism</a></div>
<p>In 1744, the directions the Wesleys gave to the Methodist societies required them "to taste no spirituous [i.e., <a href="/wiki/Distilled_beverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Distilled beverage">distilled</a>] liquor ... unless prescribed by a physician."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Methodists later took a leading role in the <a href="/wiki/Temperance_movement" title="Temperance movement">temperance movement</a> of the 19th and early 20th centuries, and Methodism remains associated with <a href="/wiki/Teetotalism" title="Teetotalism">teetotalism</a> in the public mind.<sup id="cite_ref-alcohol_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alcohol-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Methodists saw alcoholic beverages, and <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>, as the root of many <a href="/wiki/Social_ills" class="mw-redirect" title="Social ills">social ills</a> and tried to persuade people to abstain from these.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Temperance appealed strongly to the Methodist doctrines of <a href="/wiki/Sanctification" title="Sanctification">sanctification</a> and perfection. At one time, ministers had to take a pledge not to drink and encouraged their congregations to do the same.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> To this day, alcohol remains banned in Methodist premises.<sup id="cite_ref-alcohol_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alcohol-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup> However, the choice to consume alcohol outside of church is now a personal decision for any member.<sup id="cite_ref-alcohol_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alcohol-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Methodist Church has long used non-alcoholic wine (grape juice) in the sacrament of Holy Communion.<sup id="cite_ref-alcohol_148-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alcohol-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In 1869, a Methodist dentist named <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bramwell_Welch" title="Thomas Bramwell Welch">Thomas Welch</a> developed a method of <a href="/wiki/Pasteurising" class="mw-redirect" title="Pasteurising">pasteurising</a> grape juice in order to produce an <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">unfermented</a> communion wine for his church.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> (He later founded <a href="/wiki/Welch%27s" title="Welch&#39;s">Welch's</a> grape juice company.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>) By the 1880s this non-alcoholic wine had become commonplace in Methodist churches worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poverty">Poverty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Poverty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the start Methodism was sympathetic towards poor people. In 1753, John Wesley bemoaned, "So wickedly, devilishly false is that common objection, ‘They are poor, only because they are idle’."<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> In a <a href="/wiki/Joint_Public_Issues_Team" title="Joint Public Issues Team">Joint Public Issues Team</a> report issued with the <a href="/wiki/Baptist_Union_of_Great_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptist Union of Great Britain">Baptist Union of Great Britain</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Reformed_Church" title="United Reformed Church">United Reformed Church</a>, the Methodist Church regret this misconception is also prevalent today.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Daleep_Mukarji&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Daleep Mukarji (page does not exist)">Daleep Mukarji</a>, Methodist Vice-President in 2013 and former Director of the charity <a href="/wiki/Christian_Aid" title="Christian Aid">Christian Aid</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> said <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a> was more prevalent in 21st century Britain than at any time since <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Twenty-five per cent of British residents experience <a href="/wiki/Relative_deprivation" title="Relative deprivation">relative poverty</a>, disproportionately many are children.<sup id="cite_ref-MethodistConference2013_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MethodistConference2013-158">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Working with others, people of faith or no faith, we need to work for justice, inclusion and development that benefits the poor and marginalised here in the UK and across the world. This requires that we be prepared for the education, organisation and equipping of our members so that we build the necessary energy and commitment to see changes in our society. (...) We must hold our leaders, the structures and systems accountable so that we see that the weak and vulnerable are given a better deal. (...)  Many Methodists in our local churches and circuits have outstanding programmes that serve people in need. At this time when poverty, deprivation and neglect seem to have got worse we should do more. (...)  Our Methodist church is known for our service, our commitment to social justice and our willingness to act to transform society.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Daleep Mukarji<sup id="cite_ref-MethodistConference2013_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MethodistConference2013-158">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ministry">Ministry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Ministry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presbyters_and_deacons">Presbyters and deacons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Presbyters and deacons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2016 there were 3,459 Methodist ministers, with 1,562 active in circuit ministry.<sup id="cite_ref-Piggot_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piggot-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The church recognises two orders of ordained ministry—that of <a href="/wiki/Presbyter" title="Presbyter">presbyter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deacon" title="Deacon">deacon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ministry1_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ministry1-159">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup>
Church documents refer to both as "<a href="/wiki/Minister_(Christianity)" title="Minister (Christianity)">Minister</a>", though popular usage often limits this title to presbyters.<sup id="cite_ref-ministry1_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ministry1-159">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Presbyters are styled "<a href="/wiki/The_Reverend" title="The Reverend">The Reverend</a>", while "Deacon" is used as a title by members of the diaconate. Deacons (both men and women) also belong to a community of deacons in the <a href="/wiki/Methodist_diaconal_order" title="Methodist diaconal order">Methodist diaconal order</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MDC1_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MDC1-163">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The Deed of Union (the key foundation document of the Methodist Church since it became united in 1932<sup id="cite_ref-Methodist_Church_Act_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Methodist_Church_Act-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>) describes the roles of presbyters and deacons and the purpose of their ministries:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Christ's ministers in the church are stewards in the household of God and shepherds of his flock. Some are called and ordained to this occupation as presbyters or deacons. Presbyters have a principal and directing part in these great duties but they hold no priesthood differing in kind from that which is <a href="/wiki/Universal_priesthood" title="Universal priesthood">common to all the Lord's people</a> and they have no exclusive title to the preaching of the gospel or the care of souls. These ministries are shared with them by others to whom also the Spirit divides his gifts severally as he wills.<sup id="cite_ref-deed_of_union_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deed_of_union-127">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Wesley._Stipple_engraving_after_W._Hamilton,_1788._Wellcome_V0006238.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/John_Wesley._Stipple_engraving_after_W._Hamilton%2C_1788._Wellcome_V0006238.jpg/170px-John_Wesley._Stipple_engraving_after_W._Hamilton%2C_1788._Wellcome_V0006238.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/John_Wesley._Stipple_engraving_after_W._Hamilton%2C_1788._Wellcome_V0006238.jpg/255px-John_Wesley._Stipple_engraving_after_W._Hamilton%2C_1788._Wellcome_V0006238.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/John_Wesley._Stipple_engraving_after_W._Hamilton%2C_1788._Wellcome_V0006238.jpg/340px-John_Wesley._Stipple_engraving_after_W._Hamilton%2C_1788._Wellcome_V0006238.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2362" data-file-height="3007" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Wesley._Stipple_engraving_after_W._Hamilton,_1788._Wellcome_V0006238.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Wesley ordained and sent forth every Methodist preacher in his day.</div></div></div>
<p>The two orders are equal in status, with presbyters and deacons each serving a distinct role in the ministry of the Methodist Church. Deacons are called to a ministry of service and <a href="/wiki/Evangelism" title="Evangelism">witness</a>: specifically to "assist God's people in worship and prayer" and "to visit and support the sick and the suffering".<sup id="cite_ref-MDC1_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MDC1-163">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Presbyters are called to a ministry of word and sacrament: "to preach by word and deed the Gospel of God's grace" and "to baptise, to confirm, and to preside at the celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">sacrament of Christ's body and blood</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-MDC1_163-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MDC1-163">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Presbyters are <a href="/wiki/Itinerant_preacher" title="Itinerant preacher">itinerant preachers</a>, residing in a circuit for usually five years before transferring to another.
</p><p>Methodist presbyters are usually given <a href="/wiki/Pastoral_charge" title="Pastoral charge">pastoral charge</a> of several <a href="/wiki/Local_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Local church">local churches</a> in a circuit. Ordinary presbyters are in turn overseen by a <a href="/wiki/Superintendent_(ecclesiastical)" title="Superintendent (ecclesiastical)">superintendent</a>, who is the most senior minister in a circuit. Unlike many other Methodist denominations the British church does not have <a href="/wiki/Bishop_(Methodism)" title="Bishop (Methodism)">bishops</a>. A report, <i>What Sort of Bishops?</i> to the conference of 2005, was accepted for study and report.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> This report considered whether this should now be changed, and if so, what forms of <a href="/wiki/Episcopacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Episcopacy">episcopacy</a> might be acceptable. Consultation at grassroots level during 2006 and 2007 revealed overwhelming opposition from those who responded. As a consequence, the 2007 conference decided not to move towards having bishops at present.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Without bishops, the Methodist Church does not subscribe to the idea of an <a href="/wiki/Historical_episcopate" title="Historical episcopate">historical episcopate</a>. It does, however, affirm the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_succession" title="Apostolic succession">apostolic succession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jay_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jay-166">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> In 1937 the Methodist Conference located the "true continuity" with the church of past ages in "the continuity of Christian experience, the fellowship in the gift of the one Spirit; in the continuity in the allegiance to one Lord, the continued proclamation of the message; the continued acceptance of the mission;..." [through a long chain which goes back to] "the first disciples in the company of the Lord Himself ... This is our doctrine of apostolic succession" [which neither depends on, nor is secured by,] "an official succession of ministers, whether bishops or presbyters, from apostolic times, but rather by fidelity to apostolic truth".<sup id="cite_ref-Jay_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jay-166">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ordination_of_women">Ordination of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Ordination of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women_in_Methodism" title="Ordination of women in Methodism">Ordination of women in Methodism</a></div>
<p>John Wesley allowed female preachers.  The Primitive Methodist Church always allowed female preachers and ministers, although there were never many of them.  The Wesleyan Methodist Church established an order of <a href="/wiki/Deaconess" title="Deaconess">deaconesses</a> in 1890. The Methodist Church has re-allowed <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women" title="Ordination of women">ordination of women</a> as presbyters since 2 July 1974, at the Methodist Conference in Bristol.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> The Methodist Church, along with some other Protestant churches, holds that when the historical contexts involved are understood, a coherent biblical argument can be made in favour of women's ordination.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_preachers">Local preachers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Local preachers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Methodist_local_preacher" title="Methodist local preacher">Methodist local preacher</a></div>
<p>A distinctive feature of British Methodism is its extensive use of "local preachers" ('local' because they stay in the same circuit, as opposed to 'itinerant' preachers who move to different circuits, in the case of presbyters).<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> They are <a href="/wiki/Laity" title="Laity">laypeople</a> or deacons who have been trained and accredited to preach and lead worship services in place of a presbyter; however, local preachers cannot ordinarily officiate at services of Holy Communion.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Local preachers are thus similar to <a href="/wiki/Lay_reader" title="Lay reader">lay readers</a> in the Church of England.<sup id="cite_ref-local_preacher2_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-local_preacher2-171">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>  It is estimated that local preachers conduct seven out of every ten Methodist services, either in their own circuit or in others where they are invited as "visiting preachers".<sup id="cite_ref-local_preacher2_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-local_preacher2-171">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> All candidates for ordination as a presbyter in the Methodist Church are required to be admitted as local preachers before they can be accepted as candidates or begin their training.<sup id="cite_ref-local_preacher2_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-local_preacher2-171">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Local preachers have played an important role in English and Welsh social history, especially among the working class and <a href="/wiki/Labour_movement" title="Labour movement">Labour movement</a>. Recent prominent public figures who preached include <a href="/wiki/George_Thomas,_1st_Viscount_Tonypandy" title="George Thomas, 1st Viscount Tonypandy">George Thomas</a>, Speaker of the House of Commons from 1976 to 1983,<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Len_Murray" class="mw-redirect" title="Len Murray">Len Murray</a>, General Secretary of the <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">Trades Union Congress</a> from 1973 to 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation">Organisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Organisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Governance_of_the_Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain" title="Governance of the Methodist Church of Great Britain">Governance of the Methodist Church of Great Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connexionalism" title="Connexionalism">Connexionalism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chellastonmethodist.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Chellastonmethodist.JPG/220px-Chellastonmethodist.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Chellastonmethodist.JPG/330px-Chellastonmethodist.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Chellastonmethodist.JPG/440px-Chellastonmethodist.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2176" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chellastonmethodist.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical Methodist chapel in <a href="/wiki/Chellaston" title="Chellaston">Chellaston</a>. Local churches are grouped into circuits to which ministers are appointed.</div></div></div>
<p>Methodists belong to local churches (usually meeting in a building called a "<a href="/wiki/Chapel" title="Chapel">chapel</a>"), or <a href="/wiki/Local_ecumenical_partnership" title="Local ecumenical partnership">local ecumenical partnerships</a>, but also feel part of a larger connected community, known as <i>The Connexion</i>. This sense of being connected makes a difference to how the Methodist Church as a whole is structured. From its inception under John Wesley, Methodism has always laid strong emphasis on the <a href="/wiki/Interdependence" class="mw-redirect" title="Interdependence">interdependence</a> and mutual support, in terms of ministry and finance, of one local church for another.<sup id="cite_ref-Catechism_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catechism-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The church community has never been seen in isolation either from its immediately neighbouring church communities or from the centralised national organisation. When ministers are ordained in the Methodist Church, they are also "received into full Connexion."<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_churches">Local churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Local churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haroldswick_Methodist_Church_-_geograph.org.uk_-_311241.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Haroldswick_Methodist_Church_-_geograph.org.uk_-_311241.jpg/220px-Haroldswick_Methodist_Church_-_geograph.org.uk_-_311241.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Haroldswick_Methodist_Church_-_geograph.org.uk_-_311241.jpg/330px-Haroldswick_Methodist_Church_-_geograph.org.uk_-_311241.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Haroldswick_Methodist_Church_-_geograph.org.uk_-_311241.jpg/440px-Haroldswick_Methodist_Church_-_geograph.org.uk_-_311241.jpg 2x" data-file-width="593" data-file-height="394" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haroldswick_Methodist_Church_-_geograph.org.uk_-_311241.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Methodist chapel in <a href="/wiki/Haroldswick" title="Haroldswick">Haroldswick</a> is the northernmost church in Britain. Methodism has a strong presence in the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Shetland Islands">Shetland Islands</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Membership of the Methodist Church is held in a particular local church, or in a local ecumenical partnership.<sup id="cite_ref-members_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-members-175">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> For people who wish to become members of the church there is a period of training and, once the local church council is satisfied with the person's sincere acceptance of the basis of membership of the Methodist Church, a service of <a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">confirmation</a> and reception into membership is held; if they have not previously been baptised, the service will include baptism.<sup id="cite_ref-members_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-members-175">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> As at October 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, church members are dispersed over 4,512 local churches—unevenly distributed over a small number of large churches and a large number of small churches.<sup id="cite_ref-Piggot_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piggot-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Local church</i> can refer to both the <a href="/wiki/Church_congregation" class="mw-redirect" title="Church congregation">congregation</a> and the building.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> It is the whole body of members of the Methodist Church linked with one particular place of worship. The concept of the local church is based on the original Methodist "societies" that existed within the Church of England during the time of John Wesley's ministry.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> A local church is normally led by a presbyter, usually referred to as "the minister".
</p><p>Some church members belong to a church council, either because they have been elected by the local church members, or because they hold one of a number of offices within the local church. The church council, with a minister, has responsibility for running the local church. Members of the church council are also <a href="/wiki/Trustee" title="Trustee">trustees</a> of the local church.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circuits">Circuits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Circuits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Local churches are grouped into 368 <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Circuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist Circuit">circuits</a> (as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>) of various sizes.<sup id="cite_ref-Piggot_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piggot-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The responsibilities of the circuit are exercised through the circuit meeting, led by the superintendent minister.<sup id="cite_ref-Circuit_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Circuit-179">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> It is responsible for managing the finances, property and officeholders within the circuit. Most circuits have many fewer ministers than churches and the majority of services are led by local preachers, or by <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/supernumerary" class="extiw" title="wikt:supernumerary">supernumerary</a> ministers—retired ministers who are not officially counted in the number of ministers for the circuit in which they are listed.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The superintendent and other ministers are assisted in the leadership and administration of the circuit by lay circuit stewards, who together form the leadership team.<sup id="cite_ref-Circuit_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Circuit-179">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_halls">Central halls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Central halls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:December_Methodist_Center_Hall_London_Her_Majesty_Services_Evangelize_Genever_-_Master_England_Photography_2012_-_panoramio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/December_Methodist_Center_Hall_London_Her_Majesty_Services_Evangelize_Genever_-_Master_England_Photography_2012_-_panoramio.jpg/250px-December_Methodist_Center_Hall_London_Her_Majesty_Services_Evangelize_Genever_-_Master_England_Photography_2012_-_panoramio.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/December_Methodist_Center_Hall_London_Her_Majesty_Services_Evangelize_Genever_-_Master_England_Photography_2012_-_panoramio.jpg/375px-December_Methodist_Center_Hall_London_Her_Majesty_Services_Evangelize_Genever_-_Master_England_Photography_2012_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/December_Methodist_Center_Hall_London_Her_Majesty_Services_Evangelize_Genever_-_Master_England_Photography_2012_-_panoramio.jpg/500px-December_Methodist_Center_Hall_London_Her_Majesty_Services_Evangelize_Genever_-_Master_England_Photography_2012_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3973" data-file-height="2974" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:December_Methodist_Center_Hall_London_Her_Majesty_Services_Evangelize_Genever_-_Master_England_Photography_2012_-_panoramio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Central_Hall,_Westminster" title="Methodist Central Hall, Westminster">Methodist Central Hall</a>, Westminster, serves as a church and conference centre.</div></div></div>
<p>Some large inner-city churches, known as 'central halls', are designated as circuits in themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Stats1_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stats1-182">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> About a hundred such halls were built in Britain between 1886 and 1945, many in a <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance</a> or <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque</a> style.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> They were designated as multi-purpose venues; in their heyday they presented low-cost concerts and shows to entertain the working classes on Saturdays, encouraging them to <a href="/wiki/Temperance_movement" title="Temperance movement">abstain from alcohol</a>, as well as hosting church congregations on Sundays. However, many were bombed during the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, and others declined as people moved out of the city centres; as of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> only sixteen remain in use as Methodist churches.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-184">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Others, such as the landmark <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Central_Hall,_Birmingham" title="Methodist Central Hall, Birmingham">Birmingham Central Hall</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Hall,_Liverpool" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Central Hall, Liverpool">Liverpool's Grand Central Hall</a>, have been sold and adapted as retail or nightclub venues.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-184">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> One of the remaining halls is <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Central_Hall,_Westminster" title="Methodist Central Hall, Westminster">Methodist Central Hall</a>, located off <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Square" title="Parliament Square">Parliament Square</a> in <a href="/wiki/Westminster" title="Westminster">Westminster</a>, established in 1912 to serve as a church with additional use "for conferences on religious, educational, scientific, philanthropic and social questions".<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Districts">Districts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Districts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Connexion is divided into thirty-one districts covering the whole of Great Britain, the Isle of Man and the Channel Islands.<sup id="cite_ref-Stats1_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stats1-182">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The district is a drawing together of a variable number of circuits in a geographic locality. Methodism has never been prevalent in Scotland and there are only around 40 local churches gathered into one Scotland District.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The governing body of a district is the twice-yearly <a href="/wiki/Synod" title="Synod">synod</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Each district is presided over by a <a href="/wiki/Chairman" class="mw-redirect" title="Chairman">chair</a>, except the large London District which has three chairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Chairs_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chairs-188">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> A chair was, at first, a superintendent of a circuit within the district, but now ministers are appointed exclusively to the role.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The prime function of the chair is <a href="/wiki/Pastoral_care" title="Pastoral care">pastoral</a>—the care of ministers, and lay workers, and their families, within the district; the appointment of ministers to circuits; candidates for the ministry and the oversight of probationer (trainee) ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-Chairs_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chairs-188">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The district chair is also the person to whom other denominations relate ecumenically at regional or national level.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Annual_Conference">Annual Conference</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Annual Conference">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_Methodist_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of the Methodist Conference">List of Presidents of the Methodist Conference</a></div>
<p>The central governing body of the Connexion is the Methodist Conference which meets in June each year in a different part of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Conference_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conference-191">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> It represents both ministers and laypeople, and determines church policy.<sup id="cite_ref-Conference_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conference-191">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The Conference is a gathering of representatives from each district, along with some who have been elected by the Conference and some <a href="/wiki/Ex_officio" class="mw-redirect" title="Ex officio">ex officio</a> members and representatives of the youth assembly. It is held in three sessions, for the presbyterate, the diaconate and a representative session including lay representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-Conference_191-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conference-191">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Methodist Conference is the formal authority on all matters of belief and practice.<sup id="cite_ref-reports_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reports-192">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Proposals for a change or development of Methodist teaching about personal, social or public <a href="/wiki/Christian_ethics" title="Christian ethics">Christian ethics</a> can be initiated:
</p>
<ol><li>by any two representatives to the annual Conference proposing a resolution (known as a Notice of Motion) at the Conference itself;</li>
<li>by local groupings of churches (circuit meetings) by regional groupings of churches (synods) proposing a resolution to the Conference;</li>
<li>by a resolution to Conference from the Methodist Council (a smaller representative body which meets four times a year between Conferences).</li></ol>
<p>If, by methods 1 and 2 above, the proposed change or development is significant, the Conference will usually direct the Methodist Council to look into the issues and to present a report at a subsequent Conference.<sup id="cite_ref-reports_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reports-192">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the course of preparing the report, staff who are appointed or employed by the Council will be responsible for developing the church's thinking with the help of professional and <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theological</a> expertise; and must undertake a wide range of consultations, both within the Methodist Church and with partner denominations. Then the report, with or without specific recommendations, will be presented to Conference for debate.
</p><p>Examples of issues dealt with in this way are: abortion; civil disobedience; nuclear deterrence; the manufacture and sale of arms; disarmament; care of the environment; family and divorce law; gambling; housing; overseas development and fair trading; poverty; racial justice; asylum and immigration issues; human sexuality; political responsibility.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sometimes the Conference will attempt a definitive judgement on an important theme which is intended to represent the Methodist Church's viewpoint for a decade or more. In such cases a final decision is made after two debates in Conference, separated by at least a year, to allow for discussion in all parts of the church's life. Topics of personal, social or public Christian ethics dealt with in this way become official "Statements" or "Declarations" of the Methodist Church on the subject concerned, for example, <i>Family Life, the Single Person and Marriage</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-family92_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-family92-137">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>The Constitutional Practice and Discipline of the Methodist Church</i> (CPD) is published annually by order of the Conference. Its contents are prepared by the Church's Law and Polity Committee and reviewed each year. Volume 1 contains a set of fixed texts, including Acts of Parliament, other legislation and historic documents; volume 2 includes the Deed of Union and Model Trusts, along with standing orders which are updated annually after amendments by Conference.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Methodist Conference is presided over by the President of Conference, a presbyter, who is currently Barbara Glasson (as of July 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-presidency_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidency-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The President is supported by the Vice-President, who is a layperson or deacon. The President and Vice-President serve a one-year term, travelling across the Connexion—following the example of Wesley—and preaching at local churches.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Charities">Charities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Charities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Methodist Church is closely associated with several charitable organisations: namely, <a href="/wiki/Action_for_Children" title="Action for Children">Action for Children</a> (formerly the National Children's Home),<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Homes" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist Homes">Methodist Homes</a> and  <a href="/wiki/All_We_Can" title="All We Can">All We Can</a> (the Methodist Relief and Development Fund).<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
The church also helps to run a number of <a href="/wiki/Faith_school" title="Faith school">faith schools</a>, both state and independent. These include two leading public schools in East Anglia, <a href="/wiki/Culford_School" title="Culford School">Culford School</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Leys" class="mw-redirect" title="The Leys">The Leys</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> It helps to promote an all round education with a strong <a href="/wiki/Religious_education#United_Kingdom" title="Religious education">Christian ethos</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecumenical_and_interfaith_relations">Ecumenical and interfaith relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Ecumenical and interfaith relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglican_and_Methodist_Church_of_St_Matthew,_Rastrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_519716.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Anglican_and_Methodist_Church_of_St_Matthew%2C_Rastrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_519716.jpg/250px-Anglican_and_Methodist_Church_of_St_Matthew%2C_Rastrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_519716.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Anglican_and_Methodist_Church_of_St_Matthew%2C_Rastrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_519716.jpg/375px-Anglican_and_Methodist_Church_of_St_Matthew%2C_Rastrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_519716.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Anglican_and_Methodist_Church_of_St_Matthew%2C_Rastrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_519716.jpg/500px-Anglican_and_Methodist_Church_of_St_Matthew%2C_Rastrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_519716.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglican_and_Methodist_Church_of_St_Matthew,_Rastrick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_519716.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>St Matthew Church, <a href="/wiki/Rastrick" title="Rastrick">Rastrick</a>, is an example of a local ecumenical partnership where Methodists and <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglicans</a> work and worship together as one congregation.</div></div></div>
